| [Lew](https://infosec.exchange/@pythonshell) | [12/22/2022 09:24:30](https://infosec.exchange/@pythonshell/109556601373229086) |                                                                                                               <p>One of the most useful tools within PowerShell is the Group-Object command. You can use this to perform basic data analysis and see the distribution of data. For example, I will use Get-MessageTrace and Get-AzureADAuditLogs and then group by IP Address to see whether there have been multiple logins/sends from known/unknown IP addresses. You can then work backwards to identify and block Malicious IP addresses. This can be used if you want to double-check your logins for validity. <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/powershell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>powershell</span></a></p>                                                                                                               |

| [Mike Kanakos](https://fosstodon.org/@mikekanakos) | [12/16/2022 03:47:32](https://fosstodon.org/@mikekanakos/109521302517563951) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               <p><span class="h-card"><a href="https://mstdn.social/@cjerrington" class="u-url mention">@<span>cjerrington</span></a></span> <span class="h-card"><a href="https://techhub.social/@JeffHicks" class="u-url mention">@<span>JeffHicks</span></a></span> </p><p>From Reddit:<br />&quot;Short version, Start-Process has the option to do more.</p><p>You can start programs as admin, you can pass additional parameters to an executable, send stdout to a file when you&#39;re calling a .bat, and more. Those options aren&#39;t available using Invoke-Item.</p><p>Trade off for the ability to do more is usually greater memory and/or CPU utilization.&quot;<br /><a href="https://fosstodon.org/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a>  <br /><a href="https://www.reddit.com/r/PowerShell/comments/9pfhm3/invokeitem_vs_startprocess/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://www.</span><span class="ellipsis">reddit.com/r/PowerShell/commen</span><span class="invisible">ts/9pfhm3/invokeitem_vs_startprocess/</span></a></p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               |

| [Jeff Hicks üê∂üéºüç∑üñ•Ô∏è](https://techhub.social/@JeffHicks) | [12/15/2022 22:59:48](https://techhub.social/@JeffHicks/109520171055741040) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   <p>I love being able to launch graphical Linux tools from WSL in Windows from a <a href="https://techhub.social/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a> prompt.</p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   |
| [Jasey DePriest (she / her) :donor:](she / her) | [12/15/2022 22:04:09](https://infosec.exchange/@jrdepriest/109519952237071129) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    <p>While looking for a better way to get the <em>best</em> <code>lastLogon</code> time from AD, I found this TechNet Blog Post about <a href="https://social.technet.microsoft.com/wiki/contents/articles/22461.understanding-the-ad-account-attributes-lastlogon-lastlogontimestamp-and-lastlogondate.aspx" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer">LastLogon, LastLogonTimeStamp, and LastLogonDate</a><br>As some of you probably already know, the <code>lastLogon</code> value is not replicated. It is only updated on the specific DC that authenticates a user for any given session. <code>LastLogonTimeStamp</code> <strong>is</strong> replicated but, by default, it can be up to 14 or more days out of date.<br>So I wanted to query every single domain controller for the value then compare them and spit out the most recent one.<br>That blog post has a one-liner for getting all the <code>lastLogon</code> values:</p><pre><code>$(foreach ($DC in ((get-addomaincontroller -filter * | sort name).name) ){ $user = get-aduser chris -properties lastlogon -server $dc | select name,lastlogon ; echo "$DC - $(w32tm /ntte $user.lastlogon)" }  )<br></code></pre><p>It takes about 5 seconds to run.<br>Where <code>chris</code> is the Identity you are searching for.<br>You could easily sort it and pick the most recent.<br>Unfortunately, I need to do this for thousands of user IDs across a dozen domain controllers.<br>So I created an update using <code>-Parallel</code>. You also need to set a <code>SamAccountName</code> to search for.</p><pre><code>$DCList = (Get-ADDomainController -Filter *).Name<br>$BestLastLogon = (($DCList | ForEach-Object -Parallel {<br>    $DC = $_;<br>    (Get-AdUser -Server $DC -Identity $using:SamAccountName -Properties lastLogon).lastLogon<br>} -ThrottleLimit 24) | Sort-Object -Desc)[0]<br>$BestLastLogon<br>$(w32tm /ntte $BestLastLogon)<br></code></pre><p>This takes about 1.5 seconds to run.</p><p>The version I use is in my production script is more complicated because I needed to account for a DC timing out and then retrying, but you get the idea.</p><p>I really want to wrap this up into a function, but I keep running into errors so I thought I'd post it here.</p><p><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/powershell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>powershell</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/lastlogon" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>lastlogon</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/activedirectory" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>activedirectory</span></a></p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |
| [James Brundage](https://fosstodon.org/@MrPowerShell) | [12/15/2022 22:01:29](https://fosstodon.org/@MrPowerShell/109519941742323381) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    <p>New bits of Irregular are out <a href="https://github.com/StartAutomating/Irregular" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">github.com/StartAutomating/Irr</span><span class="invisible">egular</span></a>:</p><p>New in this version:</p><p>* ?&lt;Code_Method&gt; (Matches methods from almost any language)<br />* ?&lt;Code_PackageVersion&gt; (Matches package names and versions)<br />* ?&lt;ANSI_Link&gt; (Match hyperlinks in ANSI).</p><p>If you use <a href="https://fosstodon.org/tags/RegEx" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>RegEx</span></a> and <a href="https://fosstodon.org/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a>, check out Irregular and see how it makes Regular Expressions Strangely Simple.</p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    |

| [Wes Lambert](https://infosec.exchange/@weslambert) | [12/11/2022 16:41:52](https://infosec.exchange/@weslambert/109496035728710233) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     <p>ü¶ñDay 81 of the <span class="h-card"><a href="https://infosec.exchange/@velocidex" class="u-url mention">@<span>velocidex</span></a></span> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/velociraptor" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>velociraptor</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/ArtifactsOfAutumn" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>ArtifactsOfAutumn</span></a> series  </p><p>Artifact: Windows\.Persistence\.PowershellRegistry</p><p>Link: <a href="https://docs.velociraptor.app/artifact_references/pages/windows.persistence.powershellregistry" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">docs.velociraptor.app/artifact</span><span class="invisible">_references/pages/windows.persistence.powershellregistry</span></a></p><p>----</p><p>A common way of persistence is to install a hook into a user profile registry hive, using PowerShell. When the user logs in, the PowerShell script downloads a payload and executes it.</p><p><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1112/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1</span><span class="invisible">112/</span></a><br /><a href="https://attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1547/001/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">attack.mitre.org/techniques/T1</span><span class="invisible">547/001/</span></a></p><p>----</p><p>This artifact searches user registry hives for signatures related to general Powershell execution. </p><p>A YARA signature is used to target the user‚Äôs profile, which is extracted using raw NTFS parsing, in case the user is currently logged on and the registry hive is locked.</p><p>----</p><p>Once detected, a row is returned and the registry hive is uploaded to the Velociraptor server.</p><p>In the provided images, we can see an example using mshta\.exe to execute code.</p><p><a href="https://redcanary.com/threat-detection-report/techniques/mshta" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">redcanary.com/threat-detection</span><span class="invisible">-report/techniques/mshta</span></a> </p><p>----</p><p>That&#39;s it for now! Stay tuned to learn about more artifacts! ü¶ñ</p><p>If you would like to learn more about this technique and others, check out the link below, as well as it&#39;s references!</p><p><a href="https://docs.velociraptor.app/blog/html/2018/09/29/detecting_powershell_persistence_with_velociraptor_and_yara/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer"><span class="invisible">https://</span><span class="ellipsis">docs.velociraptor.app/blog/htm</span><span class="invisible">l/2018/09/29/detecting_powershell_persistence_with_velociraptor_and_yara/</span></a></p><p><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/DFIR" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>DFIR</span></a> <br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/Forensics" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>Forensics</span></a> <br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/Infosec" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>Infosec</span></a><br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a><br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/ThreatHunting" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>ThreatHunting</span></a><br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/Windows" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>Windows</span></a></p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     |

In <a href="https://tech.lgbt/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a> don&#39;t do:</p><p>$a= , [object]::new() * $dimension</p><p>But:</p><p>$a= [object[]]::new($dimension)<br />for ($i=0; $i -lt $dimension; $i++) {<br />    $a[$i] = [object]::new()<br />}</p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        |

| [HaircutFish](https://infosec.exchange/@haircutfish) | [12/02/2022 18:50:03](https://infosec.exchange/@haircutfish/109445579007001664) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  <p>Working on some PowerShell today, as I continue to go through the Udemy PowerShell course. Learning about Objects and methods, not only how to use some of them but if you are not sure how to use them where to find how to use them. </p><p>Like let&#39;s take for example if you wanted to get the BIOS version of your PC. You would start with the cmdlet help Get-WmiObject -ShowWindow. Then you could scroll down through the window to see different examples, one of them being -Class Win32_Bios.  So then we use the cmdlet Get-WmiObject -Class Win_32_Bios | gm . The gm is the alias for Get-Member, which will list out the different properties and methods that can be run with the cmdlet. So the property we are looking for is BIOSVersion, so now we can build up the full cmdlet.  Get-WmiObject -Class Win32_Bios | Select-Object BIOSVersion , once we run this command in PowerShell we will be given our BIOS version. Awesome stuff!!!<br /><a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/Udemy" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>Udemy</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/NeverStopLearning" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>NeverStopLearning</span></a> <a href="https://infosec.exchange/tags/PropertyandMethod" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PropertyandMethod</span></a></p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  |

| [Joel Bennett](https://fosstodon.org/@jaykul) | [12/02/2022 05:02:02](https://fosstodon.org/@jaykul/109442323090210063) |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                <p>I have a trick for faster <a href="https://fosstodon.org/tags/PowerShell" class="mention hashtag" rel="tag">#<span>PowerShell</span></a> profiles:</p><p>1. Put the configuration for your prompt and PSReadLine into a separate file. **Not** in your in your profile. Let&#39;s call it interactive.ps1</p><p>2. Make sure you set your prompt at the bottom of interactive.ps1, and call it.</p><p>3. At the bottom of your profile, put:<br /> <br />function prompt {<br />&amp; $PSScriptRoot/interactive.ps1<br />}</p><p>Now your readline &amp; prompt customization only run when you need them, and don&#39;t slow non-interactive use like `pwsh -c`</p>                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                |
